                .build(),
            interstitial: googletag.sizeMapping()
                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202302613')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="d5130b2eb9524371a5fed637dbd2999b"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d5130b2eb9524371a5fed637dbd2999b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '52168088-e9d3-4e26-b637-63952b3e8d62'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '508C8EE1C8FFC196A75F9F1C153E76EA';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d74221598" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon anodes with all-plateau capacities below 0.1&nbsp;V are prerequisites to achieve high-energy-density sodium-ion storage, which holds promise for future sustainable energy technologies. However, challenges in removing defects and improving the insertion of sodium ions head off the development of hard carbon to achieve this goal. Herein, a highly cross-linked topological graphitized carbon using biomass corn cobs through a two-step rapid thermal-annealing strategy is reported. The topological graphitized carbon constructed with long-range graphene nanoribbons and cavities/tunnels provides a multidirectional insertion of sodium ions whilst eliminating defects to absorb sodium ions at the high voltage region. Evidence from advanced techniques including in situ XRD, in situ Raman, and in situ/ex situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) indicates that the sodium ions' insertion and Na cluster formation occurred between curved topological graphite layers and in the topological cavity of adjacent graphite band entanglements. The reported topological insertion mechanism enables outstanding battery performance with a single full low-voltage plateau capacity of 290&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is almost 97% of the total capacity.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202302613-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202302613-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are deemed to be the most promising technologies for large-scale energy storages due to their economic advantages and sustainable natural reservation of raw materials, whilst requiring efforts to develop advanced anode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0001_R_d74221582e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adma202302613-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0003_R_d74221582e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Science">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the high specific capacity (≈300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and the excellent cost effectiveness, hard carbon stands out from many anode materials for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0004_R_d74221582e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0005_R_d74221582e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon, a non-graphitized carbon, is typically described as the material consisting of graphite microcrystals, turbulent layers and nanopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0004_R_d74221582e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0006_R_d74221582e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This complex disordered structure brings both slope and plateau region during charge/discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0007_R_d74221582e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>-<a href="#adma202302613-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0010_R_d74221582e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Energy Lett.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is a common understanding that a low discharge plateau of anodes is very important to achieve high output voltage, resulting in high-energy-density/high-power-density batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0011_R_d74221582e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0012_R_d74221582e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The typical hard carbon, however, has ≈30% capacity provided from high-voltage slope regions, which limit the energy density and become a challenge for practical full cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0013_R_d74221582e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, achieving all plateau carbon anode with high capacity is the critical factor for the development of the next-generation SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of hard carbon for sodium storage run into a slope region above 0.1&nbsp;V and a plateau region below 0.1 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0014_R_d74221582e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Lett.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Enabling all plateau capacity below 0.1&nbsp;V theoretically needs high ratio of graphene domains for interaction and hierarchical pores for sodium ions filling, and formation of Na clusters.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0015_R_d74221582e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - NPJ Comput. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0016_R_d74221582e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the one hand, previous results indicate that the plateau capacity of hard carbon is enhanced along with the increase of the carbonization temperature, but decreases when the temperature comes too high.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0017_R_d74221582e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0008_R_d74221582e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reason is that the increase of carbonization temperature is able to reduce the amount of defects and form larger and more pseudographite domains, which promote capacities that contributed from the sodium ions' insertions to interlayers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0007_R_d74221582e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, keeping increasing the carbonization temperature narrows the interlayer spacing and prevents the sodium ions from intercalation, resulting in the decrease of the plateau capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0008_R_d74221582e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Theoretical calculations have also demonstrated that sodium ions are most likely impossible to be intercalated into graphite when the interlayer spacing is &lt;0.37&nbsp;nm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0007_R_d74221582e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0018_R_d74221582e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It has also been suggested that the hard carbon anodes sintered at a higher temperature than 1500&nbsp;°C can only exhibit a low reversible plateau-capacity. For instance, Xu et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0008_R_d74221582e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported that the plateau capacity was disappeared when the temperature was reached to 2000&nbsp;°C. Ramirez-Rico et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0019_R_d74221582e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Anal. Biol. Fate: Polynucl. Aromat. Hydrocarbons, Int. Symp., 5th">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> observed a similar phenomenon that the reversible capacity of hard carbon is only 100&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when the temperature reaches to 2000&nbsp;°C. Titiric et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0013_R_d74221582e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> carbonized sucrose to 1900&nbsp;°C and then observed a reversible capacity of only 130&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. These phenomena indicate that the thick graphite domains prepared at a high temperature can potentially block the sodium accessible channels, resulting in the decrease of sodium-storage ability. Although some reports could achieve a high ratio of plateau-capacity by tuning carbonization temperature, it cannot balance the total sodium-storage capacity and the ratio of plateau-capacity. Thus, it is urgent to address these major challenges of hard carbon to achieve all plateau sodium insertion whilst maintaining high capacity.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we demonstrate a two-step pyrolysis strategy to synthesize a high-performance carbon materials for sodium-ion batteries. These carbon materials present a highly cross-linked defect-rich carbon ((H-CDC) in the first step, following with the formation of a highly topologically graphitized carbon (H-TPGC) in the second step, which consists of a thin and twisted interleaved structure with topological cavities/tunnels. This unique carbon structure was achieved by adjusting the pyrolysis rate and temperature of the first carbonization step. The thin and twisted interleave structure allows effective sodium ions' insertion into interlayers during charge and discharge process without blocking. Moreover, the topological tunneling favors the sodium ions' insertion and formation of sodium clusters due to the large cavity. This carbon structure is beneficial for the capacity contribution with voltage plateaus below 0.1 V, and thus for achieving carbon-based sodium-ion batteries with all-plateau capacity. In addition, advanced operando techniques were used to reveal the sodium-storage properties about sodium ions' insertion and Na cluster formation in H-TPGC. More specifically, H-TPGC presents a plateau capacity of 290&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is ≈97% of the total capacity. This work describes a unique full plateau practical sodium storage, which are promising to fabricate high output voltage and high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202302613-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202302613-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202302613-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202302613-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structure of Topological Graphitized Carbon</h3>
            
            <p>In this work, we developed a two-step carbonization method with optimized temperature to achieve advanced carbon anodes by using the middle layer of finely selected corn cobs as raw material (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Specifically, the crushed corn cobs were preheated at 800&nbsp;°C with a rate of 10&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> and then further carbonized at 2000&nbsp;°C with a rate of 10&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>. Combined with the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202302613-fig-0001">1a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), it is clearly found that this carbon (named H-TPGC) consists of multiple layers of long-range twisted graphene nanoribbons interwoven in 3D. We found that this structure does not seem to correspond to the traditional “house of cards” model. Therefore, in order to explain the formation mechanism of this bending and twisting entanglement of graphitic carbon bands, the concept of topography (TP) is introduced. Specifically, one of the local pyramidal points that constitute the random continuum entanglement between 3D graphene structure may have multiple carbon layer defects (known as topological defects), such as Stone–Wales defects, single-vacancy defects, and double-vacancy defects. (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0020_R_d74221582e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Anal. Biol. Fate: Polynucl. Aromat. Hydrocarbons, Int. Symp., 5th">20</a>-<a href="#adma202302613-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0022_R_d74221582e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For better understanding, typical structural features in H-TPGC have been marked and modelled in the HRTEM image as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0001">1a</a>: 1) Randomly interleaved structures are called topological structures (region I); the voids surrounded by multiple layers of long-range graphene nanoribbons wound around each other are called topological cavities/tunnels (region II); The starting point of the multidirectional connection of long-range graphene nanoribbons is referred to as a topological junction (region III), these three structural models could observe from different views of the multilayer Mobius torus structure which shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0001">1b</a>. Moreover, the 3D model of H-TPGCs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) derived by the HRTEM also demonstrate above conclusion. In detailed, the interlayer spacing of graphene (0.295–0.341&nbsp;nm) in topology I shows different gradient variations, indicating that the graphene layers are twisted and bent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0001">1c,d</a>). Two graphite nanoribbons are interconnected into a topological void (not called generalized pore) of 2.69–4.29&nbsp;nm (region II in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0001">1a</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0001">1e,f</a>). The intergraphene spacing between the walls of the topological cavity is larger than that of normal graphite due to the twisting effect, as shown by the distribution between 0.342–0.358&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0001">1g</a>). In general, the previous house of falling cards model does not correspond to the emerging results of some reports that found bent and twisted structures of hard carbon anodes from TEM analysis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0023_R_d74221582e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Carbon">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the stacked “pores” make it more difficult for this model to explain the sodium-ion transport process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0024_R_d74221582e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Carbon">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on above results, we use the topological model of the Mobius ring structure to explain the feature of the H-TPGC.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202302613-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5cc9e8fa-3756-48a7-b538-91effdb0268e/adma202302613-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5cc9e8fa-3756-48a7-b538-91effdb0268e/adma202302613-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/644339c6-5d40-4c3a-af44-72785e5d65ee/adma202302613-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5cc9e8fa-3756-48a7-b538-91effdb0268e/adma202302613-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202302613-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202302613-sec-0040-title">2.2 Controlled Regulation of the Topological Cavity of the TPGC</h3>
            
            <p>To disclose the formation mechanism of H-TPGC, the structural evolutions of the TPGC with the variation of temperatures in different steps was monitored. The results showed that the annealing temperature and pyrolysis rate in the first step affected the degree of cross-linking and defects in the pre-carbonized samples, while the higher temperature annealing step in the second step mainly affected the degree of graphitization of the final samples in terms of the length and number of layers of graphite bands (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202302613-fig-0002">2</a>), based on the corn-cob mid-layer as the raw material. To verify the degree of crosslinking defects stem from the first step sintering process, hard carbon samples were synthesized with high, medium and low crosslinking defects, denoted as H-CDC, M-CDC, and L-CDC, respectively. The microscopic observations demonstrate that both temperature and pyrolysis rate are important for the formation of the carbon structure. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0002">2a–c</a>, the M-CDC with slow pyrolysis rate (3&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> pyrolysis rate to 800&nbsp;°C) exhibits relatively ordered lattice stripes and compared to the fast pyrolysis of carbon at 10&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> (H-CDC). It can be seen from Raman that H-CDC has a higher proportion of D2 peaks, indicating its highly curled characteristics (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, when the temperature of the first step was increased to 1200&nbsp;°C, the L-CDC showed clearer multilayer graphene streaks, indicating its low crosslinking and low defect properties. When these hard carbon materials with different degrees of cross-linking and defects were heat-treated again at 2000&nbsp;°C, graphitized carbon with different degrees of topology were obtained, which were named H-TPGC, M-TPGC, and L-TPGC, respectively. Compared with H-TPGC, the HRTEM image of M-TPGC samples showed larger topological voids, while L-TPGC showed thick graphene band layers (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0002">2d–f</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6,S7</a>, Supporting Information). Above results suggest that maintaining a low temperature and fast pyrolysis rate in the first step is crucial to adjust the topology degree of the TPGCs. Of course, the process of carbonization, structure and properties of the final carbon materials will be affected by several factors such as the type of raw materials, the composition and content of impurities (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8–S12</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1,S2</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the conclusion of previous study,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0025_R_d74221582e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Fuel Process. Technol.">25</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0026_R_d74221582e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it pointed out that the raw material containing impurities such as Ni, Fe, Mn, Si, etc., would significantly catalyze the graphitization during the carbonization process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202302613-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/29347020-9734-4ba3-9bc1-3e4e3fb35148/adma202302613-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/29347020-9734-4ba3-9bc1-3e4e3fb35148/adma202302613-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/22f76e67-3fcf-4963-a6f4-a47582aee592/adma202302613-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/29347020-9734-4ba3-9bc1-3e4e3fb35148/adma202302613-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202302613-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202302613" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–f) HRTEM images of cross-linked defective carbon and topological graphitized carbon with their structural models: a)H-CDC, b) M-CDC, c)L-CDC; d)H-TPGC, e) M-TPGC, f) L-TPGC, respectively. g) XRD patterns and h) Raman spectra of different carbon materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns show that all three TPGCs exhibit a narrow (002) packet peak at ≈24° and a spike at 26°, indicating they were a composite of multiwalled carbon nanotubes and graphite (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0002">2g</a>). Compared to the other two, it can be found that the (002) sharp peaks of H-TPGC exhibit weak and splitting properties, which are calculated to represent intergraphene spacing of 0.343 and 0.336&nbsp;nm, respectively. Moreover, the HRTEM images and related test of these carbon materials further confirm a highly convoluted interlacing topological property of H-TPGC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). To relate this highly topological property, H-CDC is subjected to direct 2800&nbsp;°C heat treatment and exhibit more differentiated and high-intensity character&nbsp;(Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The Raman spectra of the three TPGCs show strong and narrow D-, G- and 2D peaks, indicating less oxygen-containing functional group defects and higher graphitization of the TPGC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0002">2h</a>). Further comparison with the Raman of MCMB, MWCNT and ordinary hard carbon L-CDC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) shows that H-TPGC has a clear tendency to differentiate into double peaks in the G-band like MWCNT, but its specific G-peak tip position is between the graphite G-band and the G-band of low-temperature hard carbon. The results of the Raman spectral volume integral peaks are very helpful for the analysis of oxygen-containing defects and structural defects in carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0027_R_d74221582e816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Batteries Supercaps">27</a>-<a href="#adma202302613-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0029_R_d74221582e816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Carbon">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>It is clear that TPGCs exhibit sharper D-band and G-band peaks than that of CDCs, accompanied by weaker D4 and D3 peaks, implying a reduction of the sp<sup>3</sup> structure of the graphene-terminated edge and the oxygen-containing functional group defects between adjacent graphene (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202302613-fig-0003">3a,b</a>). More interesting is that the G peak of H-TPGC still maintains a high Raman shift similar to that of H-CDC, and the convolution peak still has a high <i>I</i><sub>D2</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value, demonstrating that H-TPGC with high graphitization still has some structural defects. This phenomenon of G-band splitting was observed to a greater extent on some irradiated carbon graphene and carbon nanotubes and was interpreted as the result of topological defects, that is, caused by non-hexameric ring defects in the bent or twisted cone of the layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0030_R_d74221582e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0031_R_d74221582e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The evolution of the topological cavity from the pore size of the CDCs to the TPGC is further analyzed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0003">3c–f</a> with N<sub>2</sub> absorption/desorption and small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS). It can be seen that H-CDCs show abundant inhomogeneous distribution of mesopores (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0003">3c</a>). Due to the abundance of micro/mesopores and fragments such as unremoved oxygen-containing functional groups, H-CDCs exhibited a large specific surface area of 250.4&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). When the temperature rises to 2000&nbsp;°C, further reaction and cross-linking of oxygen-containing defects between the aromatic fragments of H-TPGC is accompanied by the disappearance of (≤1&nbsp;nm) micropores and topological entanglement of long-range nano graphite bands, leading to the formation of topological pore cavities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0003">3d</a>). While M-CDC exhibited a more uniform single pore size distribution, the corresponding second step of high temperature synthesized topographically graphitized carbon M-TPGC exhibited larger size topological cavities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0003">3d–f</a>). By further increasing the temperature of primary carbonization, the pores of L-CDC were melted and formed a continuous distribution, and the corresponding L-TPGC obtained from the second step of high-temperature treatment exhibited larger topological cavities and high graphitization at the edges. The pore evolution model plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0003">3g,h</a> can graphically summarize the variation of pore size of CDC to the topological cavities of TPGC as well as the degree of graphitization. In general, there is a positive correlation between the degree of cross-linking and defects in the primary carbonization products and the degree of topography of the secondary high-temperature carbonized graphitic carbon.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202302613-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d72c0895-ba3e-49d7-8124-6c50a030ffc0/adma202302613-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d72c0895-ba3e-49d7-8124-6c50a030ffc0/adma202302613-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/649d8f7c-e114-4dea-a52b-bb8469513601/adma202302613-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d72c0895-ba3e-49d7-8124-6c50a030ffc0/adma202302613-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202302613-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202302613" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Convolution Raman, c,d) BJH pore size distributions for N<sub>2</sub>, and e,f) comparison of SAXS profiles based on different cross-linked defective carbon (CDCs) and topological graphitic carbon (TPGCs). g) Model of CDCs and h) topological cavity model of TPGCs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202302613-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202302613-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the variation of sodium-storage behavior of carbon materials with the degree of graphitization and topological cavity, coin cells were assembled using metallic sodium foil as counter electrode and 1&nbsp;mol&nbsp;L<sup>−1</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate-dimethyl carbonate (1:1 v/v) as electrolyte. Galvanostatic current charge/discharge tests performed at 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> between 2.5 and −0.008&nbsp;V showed very different voltage-capacity distribution characteristics for carbon with different structures. The reversible capacities for the hard carbons of H-CDC, M-CDC, and L-CDC obtained after only one-step low-temperature heat treatment were 89, 167, and 227&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202302613-fig-0004">4a</a>), respectively. Compared to the other two-step pyrolytic carbons, the H-TPGCs achieve the great balance between the total sodium-storage capacity and the ratio of plateau-capacity because of the unique topological structure, with the largest single plateau capacity of 290&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, accounting for 97% of the reversible capacity, which was not seen in previous literatures. Previous studies<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0007_R_d74221582e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0032_R_d74221582e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - RSC Adv.">32</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0033_R_d74221582e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Commun.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and calculations<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0018_R_d74221582e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">18</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0032_R_d74221582e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - RSC Adv.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have shown that sodium ions cannot insert into graphite due to the small interlayer spacing (0.334&nbsp;nm) and low binding energy to sodium of graphite. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0004">4a</a> also shows the similar result that graphite with weak sodium-storage ability (31&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Carbon nanotubes are often used as a model for the pore structure of hard carbon for some theoretical calculations due to their curved carbon layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0034_R_d74221582e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0035_R_d74221582e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the electrochemical sodium-storage properties of actual carbon nanotubes do not exhibit low potential plateau capacity and have extremely low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). These phenomenon indicate the limitations of the conventional sodium storage in the plateau region for the pore filling mechanism. Previous reports found that the limit of sodium storage in hard carbon is determined by the number of carbon pores with appropriate size, which also implies that large topological cavities may lead to a reduction of sodium-ion storage sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0012_R_d74221582e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, thick graphene bands smooth out the distorted graphene layers, not only reducing the layer space to avoid sodium ions contact, but also hindering diffusion of sodium ions into the topological cavity. These two key features ultimately reduce the electrochemical performance of the M-TPGC, L-TPGC samples as anode for SIBs. In contrast, H-TPGC consisting of several curved graphene layers that cross each other and small topological cavities facilitates the dense insertion of sodium ions, leading to superior sodium-storage ability and cycle performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, it could be observed that the sodium was still densely inserted in the topological voids from the TEM image after 100 cycling, further verifying the super structural stability of H-TPGCs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). It is noted that H-TPGCs showed low capacity with EC/DMC as electrolyte at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> caused by the polarization of Na metal. And The rate performance of H-TPGCs was improved due to the reduction of polarization while the electrolyte was changed to DEGDME (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a,b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0036_R_d74221582e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Environ. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0037_R_d74221582e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202302613-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/74c1652e-d360-4dc5-bf73-6c97a6d4316a/adma202302613-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/74c1652e-d360-4dc5-bf73-6c97a6d4316a/adma202302613-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/24c89a16-6981-42f9-a55a-626e86026ef7/adma202302613-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/74c1652e-d360-4dc5-bf73-6c97a6d4316a/adma202302613-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202302613-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202302613" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Charge/discharge curves for the second cycles of different structural carbon: H-CDC, M-CDC, L-CDC, and MCMB based on dashed lines and H-TPGC, M-TPGC, L-TPGC, and MWCNT based on solid lines. b,c) Statistical analysis of plateau capacity vs initial Coulomb efficiency (b) and plateau percentage (c) based on hard carbon previously annealed from low to high temperature. d) Comparison of charge/discharge curves of M-TPGC, H-TPGC, and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> at a current density of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Second-cycle charge/discharge curves for full cells based on H-TPGC//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and M-TPGC//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. f) Cycling performance of H-TPGC//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell at a current density of 1&nbsp;C (300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). g) Comparison of energy density of H-TPGC with different carbon materials in full cell system matched with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The electrochemical behavior process of sodium storage in H-TPGC was further analyzed through the utilization of CV curves. The presence of a hysteresis loop in the deposition peak within the negative potential region is revealed by a negative potential scanning test down to −0.1&nbsp;V, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information). For the H-TPGC//Na cell, a pair of sharp redox peaks can be seen (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22a</a>, Supporting Information) upon potential scanning from −0.1 to 0.06&nbsp;V, which corresponds to the plating/exfoliation of metallic Na on the H-TPGC anode. The deposition peak of metal Na (−0.1&nbsp;V) and the dense insertion peak of Na<sup>+</sup> into the topological void (0.05&nbsp;V) are clearly separated, corresponding to two different electrochemical processes. In addition, a pair of sharp redox peaks can be observed at ≈0.01 and 0.1&nbsp;V when directly scanned only to 0&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22b</a>, Supporting Information). This result corresponds to the insertion and extraction behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> in the topological gap. The low potential nature of sodium inserting is susceptible to polarization on the sodium-metal side, leading to the underestimation about the capacity during the actual testing. In contrast, we also evaluate the CV curve with ether electrolytes due to their unique character that tend to provide a more accurate rate assessment, which can even reach the actual full-cell multiplicity performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c,d</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0004">4b,c</a> summarizes the relationship between the plateau capacity of hard carbon for various previous temperature treatments and the percentage of the plateau region and ICE. The blue region indicates low temperature hard carbon (&lt;1000&nbsp;°C), which generally has poor ICE and plateau capacity. As the temperature increases (1200–1600&nbsp;°C, green region), the plateau capacity and the percentage as well as and the ICE are improved to some extent. As shown in the pink region, further increasing the annealing temperature above 2000&nbsp;°C would decrease the plateau capacity and ICE of hard carbon. The observed phenomenon can be ascribed to the exceedingly high carbonization temperature, leading to a reduction in interlayer spacing and hindering further sodium intercalation. Moreover, a primary contributing factor is the formation of complex, long-range thick graphite domains at elevated temperatures. These domains were tightly surrounded by each other, blocking the diffusion pathways for sodium ions to access the topological voids within the interlayer gaps. Consequently, this leads to a decrease in the actual capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0013_R_d74221582e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As an empirical proof, we used sodium vanadium phosphate (NVP) cathode materials (specific characteristics between Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information) coupling TPGCs anodes to demonstrate their full-cell performance. The NVP//H-TPGC full cell shows an outstanding specific capacity with 260&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, two times higher than that of NVP//M-TPGC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0004">4d,e</a>). In addition, the NVP//H-TPGC full cell also showed excellent cycling performance, with a retention rate of 80% for 150 cycles at a current density of 1C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0004">4f</a>). This great cycle stability of full cell with high current density (1&nbsp;C) also demonstrate the well rate performance of H-TPGCs. To further assess the practical application potential of H-TPGC materials, common commercial hard carbon and commercial soft carbon were employed for comparative analysis. It can be deduced that, based on equivalent specific capacity performance, the energy density of full cell integrated with H-TPGC and NVP could be enhanced by ≈6% compared to commercial hard carbon and at least 25% compared to commercial soft carbon.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202302613-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202302613-sec-0060-title">2.4 Topological Cavity for Na<sup>+</sup> Insertion and Na Cluster Formation Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To further investigate the sodium-storage mechanism in the plateau region, the in situ TEM, in situ XRD and in situ Raman have been conducted. In situ TEM observations were carried out to provide further insight into the sodium-storage mechanism and visualize the microstructural evolution of the H-TPGC during the sodiation and denaturation processes. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202302613-fig-0005">5a</a> shows the experimental setup of the SIBs in a half-cell configuration, where a brass rod with a conductive adhesive acted as the working electrode and Na metal on a Pt manipulator acted as the counter electrode. Na<sub>2</sub>O naturally forms on the surface of the Na source through the residual oxygen in the TEM vacuum and is used as a solid electrolyte for sodium-ion transport. Selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) technique was employed at intervals, for high-resolution observation with the edge region of the H-TPGC during the sodiation process. The middle images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0005">5a,b</a> represent the TEM morphological changes from 1–12&nbsp;mins of H-TPGC during sodiation and desodiation. It can be seen that the H-TPGC particles become darker as the sodiation proceeds, indicating that the continuous insertion of sodium ions into the topological cavity. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0005">5b I–IV</a> show that the amount of Na accommodated in H-TPGC is increasing and the appearance of characteristic diffraction points containing metallic Na is becoming denser. Then, the ex situ TEM images were analyzed to investigate the sodium-storage behavior of H-TPGC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0005">5c–j</a>). In the initial stage of sodiation proceed (2.0–0.1&nbsp;V), the TEM images still exhibit relatively clear lattice stripes without other nanoparticles, indicating only few sodium was inserted into the interlayer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0005">5c–e</a>). When discharged to 0.06&nbsp;V, some black dots were generated and became denser with the further decrease of discharged potential to −0.01&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0005">5f–j</a>). As observed, the HRTEM image shows continuous fringes and the lattice space was corresponded to the (100) facet of sodium cluster (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0005">5k–m</a>). Overall, the entire plateau portion of H-TPGC is accompanied by the continuous dense insertion of sodium in the topological cavity and the presence of sodium–sodium intergroup that grow into metallic sodium clusters.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202302613-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6bb4888c-8494-40a0-8203-dd217f181be4/adma202302613-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6bb4888c-8494-40a0-8203-dd217f181be4/adma202302613-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/76f8f164-aa12-4915-8639-2678ffe62347/adma202302613-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6bb4888c-8494-40a0-8203-dd217f181be4/adma202302613-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202302613-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202302613" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ TEM investigation of sodium-storage mechanism in H-TPGC. a) Schematic illustration of the in situ experimental setup. TEM images show H-TPGC on brass rod as the working electrode, Na metal on Pt manipulator as the counter electrode, and Na<sub>2</sub>O on Na surface as the solid electrolyte. b) TEM images of TPGC with time (0–12&nbsp;min) and corresponding in situ SAED images of the sodization (I–V) and denaturation processes (VI–X). c–j) Ex situ TEM images and diffraction patterns of TPGC at different potentials: c) 2.0 V, d) 0.3 V, e) 0.1 V, f) 0.06 V, g) 0.02 V, h) 0.01 V, i) 0.00 V, j) −0.01 V. k,l) HRETM and filtered TEM close-up images of TPGC discharge to 0&nbsp;V; m) orange stripe spacing line plots of the plot in (l), respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, the in situ Raman spectra of the pristine H-TPGC exhibit an intense D-band at 1350&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and a G-band at 1600&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. During the initial discharge from 2 to 0.05&nbsp;V, a gradual decrease of the peak intensity of the D-band can be observed, and the peak position of the G-band exhibits a weak red shift with a significant broadening of the peak shape (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202302613-fig-0006">6a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). The shift of the G-peak position represents the result of electron doping caused by the gradual insertion and homogeneous diffusion of sodium in each layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0038_R_d74221582e1150" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Energy Lett.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the previous characterization, the topological graphitization of hard carbon leads to a bent nested ring layer with two kinds of graphene interlayer spacing, indicating sodium ions were intercalated into the bent graphene layer, result in&nbsp;extending for the interlayer of graphite (0.343 vs 0.334&nbsp;nm). The G-band splits into two distinct peaks after discharging to 0.05&nbsp;V and continues to grow with the depth of discharge. Moreover, the double-peak phenomenon derived from the G-band also appears in the 4L–2L phase of the lithium-intercalation graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0039_R_d74221582e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J phy. Chem. Lett.">39</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0040_R_d74221582e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Energy Lett.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>These results suggest that the insertion of sodium into topological graphitized carbon also occur as a multiphase feature. The high-frequency peak (1610&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) is associated with the Na-GIC boundary layer. The low-frequency peak (1575&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) indicates electron doping due to sodium insertion in the topological cavity. In addition, the G-band is observed to be continuously shifted to 1530&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in the latter part of the plateau region. The results of operando measurement based on hard carbon by Reddy et al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0038_R_d74221582e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Energy Lett.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> show that the position of the G-peak in the plateau region remains constant at 1550&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. The calculations indicate that the sodium is saturated when it is embedded within the graphene interstitial to form a component of NaC<sub>24</sub>. If the intercalation is continued within the graphene interstitial, the G-band continues to shift to lower frequencies. Therefore, sodium ions are allowed to continue the insertion when carbon forms a highly convoluted topological graphitic band structure. The red shift of the G-peak position caused by this Na cluster formation process can still be well recovered during the charging process indicating the stability of the H-TPGC structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202302613-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0cbb70e6-5cb7-4ce2-a4b2-54c8dbd47188/adma202302613-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0cbb70e6-5cb7-4ce2-a4b2-54c8dbd47188/adma202302613-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5eda8eaf-b57d-491a-ab80-76fcc256ff0d/adma202302613-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0cbb70e6-5cb7-4ce2-a4b2-54c8dbd47188/adma202302613-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202302613-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202302613" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ Raman spectra of TPGC. b) Charge/discharge curves of TPGC and c) corresponding in situ XRD contour pattern at a current density of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. d) In situ XRD corresponding to different potentials for TPGC. e) Topological tunneling Na cluster formation mechanism.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0006">6b–d</a> represent the initial cycle discharge-charge curves and the Operando XRD patterns obtained during the corresponding sodiation and desodiation process of H-TPGC. It can be clearly seen that the intensity of the (002) peak of H-TPGC changes periodically throughout the sodiation–desodiation process. Corresponding to Operando Raman, the intensity of the high-angle T-peak was decreased rapidly during 2–0.05&nbsp;V, further demonstrating the intercalation of sodium ions into the topological interlayer space. We divided the whole in situ discharge section into regions I, II, and III (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0006">6c</a>). As the H-TPGC electrode is sodiated in the front section of the plateau region (region II), the broad peak region of (002) will also move to a lower angle. This trend indicates that the vacancy between adjacent topological layers will also be occupied by sodium ions. The subsequent sodiation process (the second half of the plateau region) shows a clear divergence of the peak intensity, where the reduced intensity of the low-angle packet peak indicates further dense insertion of sodium ions in the topological gaps. The continued insertion of such large topological gaps would lead to the formation of superlattice sodium clusters through tight sodium-sodium stacking (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25–S27</a>, Supporting Information). The clearly differentiated strong peaks at high angle remain unchanged indicating that the interlayer already propped up by insertion of sodium in the previous one cannot continue to be intercalated. For comparison, we also tested the in situ XRD pattern of the usual hard carbon (represented here by L-CDC). This reversible intensity splitting was also observed in the in situ XRD pattern of L-CDC during charging and discharging process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). All the advanced characterization technique revealed that H-TPGCs have the ability to boost the sodium ions achieve “Na cluster formation” character: sodium ions insert within the topological voids between curved graphite layers or between adjacent graphite bands, and to form a multilayer arrangement within the voids form a formation of a similar slab like Na cluster (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202302613-fig-0006">6e</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Unlike H-TPGC, the in situ XRD pattern of L-CDC was divided into four parts. The presence of an extra IV part indicates that it represents another sodium-storage behavior. According to Raman test and pore size distribution characteristics, it can be concluded that there may still be some oxygen-containing functional group defects in the pore of L-CDC. These defects will accelerate a kind of adsorption of sodium ions in the last part of the platform region. By performing GITT tests on three types of carbon, H-CDC, L-CDC, and H-TPGC, it was calculated that the high oxygen carbon with low temperature pyrolysis still maintains a large diffusion coefficient in the low potential slope region. L-CDC also exhibits a certain slope and plateau in its electrochemical behavior since it is treated at 1200&nbsp;°C, which is similar to the treatment temperature of commercial hard carbon in general. The diffusion coefficients obtained by specific GITT calculations are similar to the findings of previous work,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202302613-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0035_R_d74221582e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0041_R_d74221582e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a>, <a href="#adma202302613-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202302613-bib-0042_R_d74221582e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with the first half of the plateau region decreasing and the last part of the second half showing an abrupt increase in the sodium-ion diffusion coefficient. In contrast, for the H-TPGC, the diffusion coefficient shows a decrease followed by a continuous flattening throughout the long low potential full plateau region (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). It is possible that the near absence of oxygen-containing defects in the topological voids of H-TPGC allows for Na cluster formation of sodium ions to persist within topological voids. Further comprehensive analysis of the d<i>V</i>/d<i>Q</i> and d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> data of different CDCs and further synthesized TPGCs. It can see by comparing the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> data that H-TPGC has a lower d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> peak compared to L-CDC (13 vs 49&nbsp;mV) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, H-TPGC also has a lower potential for irreversible sodium-storage sites (0.1–0.4&nbsp;vs 0.4–0.8&nbsp;V), suggesting that H-TPGC has an insertion-dominated sodium-storage behavior at higher potentials, corresponding to region I of the in situ XRD pattern. Further analysis of the d<i>V</i>/d<i>Q</i> data can also be divided into four parts. One of them, L-CDC, has a clear inflection point after region III. The appearance of region IV may be caused by some residual oxygen-containing defects inside the cavity of the hard carbon synthesized through low-temperature pyrolysis, which will be rapidly adsorbed sodium ions. Similar to the conclusions reached by GITT, d<i>V</i>/d<i>Q</i> for H-TPGC remained almost constant across the plateau region. We suggest that different sodium-storage mechanisms can be distinguished by the inflection point of the discharge curve (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30–32</a>, Supporting Information). For commercial hard carbon, the slope region I corresponds to the adsorption of oxygen-containing functional groups, while the part of region II behind the slope region corresponds to the interlayer inserted-type adsorption with large layer spacing. For the low potential region III, it corresponds to the insertion of sodium ions in the interlayer of small layer spacing. For region IV, carbon materials synthesized at lower temperatures may often still have oxygen-containing defects, such as vacancies in pores, which further promote the pore adsorption of sodium ions, thus leading to a rapid increase in the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions. In contrast, the diffusion coefficient and d<i>V</i>/d<i>Q</i> values remain constant for H-TPGC due to the absence of oxygen-containing defects in the formed topological cavity or tunnel, which allows for continuous Na cluster formation of sodium within it.</p>
            
            <p>In summary, highly defective porous H-CDC was obtained by tuning the optimized primary carbonization temperature and rate, and further high-temperature graphitization at 2000&nbsp;°C led to the synthesis of a highly cross-linked topological H-TPGC. This highly graphitized structure for sodium storage can achieve full-plateau properties. In addition, its suitable topological gap allows a continuous dense insertion of sodium ions. It can be described by a model in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a> (Supporting Information): H-TPGC exhibits larger topological windings and thus forming more suitable topological gaps around the same size space. Whereas L-TPGC has a longer-range graphite layer and its cross-linking twisting out larger gaps, sodium within such oversized pore size will result in a systematically weaker average binding energy, exhibiting a lower sodium filling fraction, as further filling will directly lead to sodium precipitation. In contrast, H-TPGC achieves ≈100% full insertion due to the suitable gap for tight twisting, which is sodium ions' insertion and Na cluster formation for the whole we elaborate.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202302613-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202302613-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We have developed a strategy to synthesize topological graphitized carbon with high graphitization using sustainable crops as precursors. This topological graphitized carbon is used for sodium-ion batteries to achieve high-capacity full-plateau characteristics, with a plateau capacity of 290&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, accounting for 97% of the total. These TPGCs with a rich intertwined topology of graphitic nanoribbons are visualized by HRTEM technique. Moreover, various advanced in situ characterizations demonstrate that this unique sodium-storage property with full plateau and low potential was caused by the sodium ions' insertion and Na cluster formation of topological cavity between the curved topological graphite layers and adjacent graphite band entanglements. As a demonstration, the full cells based on TPGC and NVP were assembled with a view to their practical application. We believe this unique topological structure will afford a new thought for designing of advanced high performance carbon anode. In addition, topological graphitized carbon with full-plateau and low-potential properties prepared from sustainable crops is a strong contender among various anode materials for SIBs and will be more fully investigated for practical application in grid-scale energy-storage systems.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
